					<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading mw-first-heading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Banana</span></h1>
							
<div id="p-lang-btn" class="vector-dropdown mw-portlet mw-portlet-lang"  >
	<input type="checkbox" id="p-lang-btn-checkbox" role="button" aria-haspopup="true" data-event-name="ui.dropdown-p-lang-btn" class="vector-dropdown-checkbox mw-interlanguage-selector" aria-label="Go to an article in another language. Available in 189 languages"   >

	</label>

						<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
					</div>
					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Tropical/subtropical edible staple, fruit</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the edible fruit. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Banana_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Banana (disambiguation)">Banana (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1218072481">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data div{background:#1f1f23!important;color:#f8f9fa}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data div{background:#1f1f23!important;color:#f8f9fa}}</style><table class="infobox" style="float: right; width: 25em;"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-above">Banana</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-image"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Bananavarieties.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Bananavarieties.jpg/250px-Bananavarieties.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="143" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Bananavarieties.jpg/375px-Bananavarieties.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/de/Bananavarieties.jpg 2x" data-file-width="387" data-file-height="221" /></a></span><div class="infobox-caption">Fruits of four different <a href="/wiki/List_of_banana_cultivars" title="List of banana cultivars">cultivars</a>. Left to right: <a href="/wiki/Cooking_banana" title="Cooking banana">plantain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_banana" title="Red banana">red banana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latundan_banana" title="Latundan banana">apple banana</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cavendish_banana" title="Cavendish banana">Cavendish banana</a></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Source plant(s)</th><td class="infobox-data"><i><a href="/wiki/Musa_(genus)" title="Musa (genus)">Musa</a></i></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Part(s) of plant</th><td class="infobox-data">Fruit</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Uses</th><td class="infobox-data">Food</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A <b>banana</b> is an elongated, edible <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a> – botanically a <a href="/wiki/Berry_(botany)" title="Berry (botany)">berry</a><sup id="cite_ref-morton_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morton-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> – produced by several kinds of large <a href="/wiki/Herbaceous" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbaceous">herbaceous</a> <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plants</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_(genus)" title="Musa (genus)">Musa</a></i>. In some countries, <a href="/wiki/Cooking_banana" title="Cooking banana">cooking bananas</a> are called plantains, distinguishing them from <b>dessert bananas</b>. The fruit is variable in size, color, and firmness, but is usually elongated and curved, with soft flesh rich in <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> covered with a <a href="/wiki/Peel_(fruit)" title="Peel (fruit)">rind</a>, which may have a variety of colors when ripe. The fruits grow upward in clusters near the top of the plant. Almost all modern edible seedless (<a href="/wiki/Parthenocarpy" title="Parthenocarpy">parthenocarp</a>) bananas come from two wild species – <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_acuminata" title="Musa acuminata">Musa acuminata</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_balbisiana" title="Musa balbisiana">Musa balbisiana</a></i>. Most cultivated bananas are <i>M. acuminata</i>, <i>M. balbisiana</i>, or hybrids of the two. 
</p><p><i>Musa</i> species are native to tropical <a href="/wiki/Indomalaya" class="mw-redirect" title="Indomalaya">Indomalaya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">Australia</a>; they were probably domesticated in <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>. They are grown in 135 countries, primarily for their fruit, and to a lesser extent to make <a href="/wiki/Banana_paper" title="Banana paper">banana paper</a> and textiles, while some are grown as <a href="/wiki/Ornamental_plant" title="Ornamental plant">ornamental plants</a>. The world's largest producers of bananas in 2022 were <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, which together accounted for approximately 26% of total production. Bananas are eaten raw or cooked in recipes varying from curries to banana chips, fritters, fruit preserves, or simply baked or steamed.
</p><p>Worldwide, there is no sharp distinction between dessert "bananas" and cooking "plantains": this works well enough in the Americas and Europe, but it breaks down in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> where many more kinds of bananas are grown and eaten. The term "banana" is applied also to other members of the genus <i>Musa</i>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Musa_coccinea" title="Musa coccinea">scarlet banana</a> (<i>Musa coccinea</i>), the <a href="/wiki/Musa_velutina" title="Musa velutina">pink banana</a> (<i>Musa velutina</i>), and the <a href="/wiki/Fe%27i_banana" title="Fe&#39;i banana">Fe'i bananas</a>. Members of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Ensete" title="Ensete">Ensete</a></i>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Ensete_glaucum" title="Ensete glaucum">snow banana</a> (<i>Ensete glaucum</i>) and the economically important <a href="/wiki/Ensete_ventricosum" title="Ensete ventricosum">false banana</a> (<i>Ensete ventricosum</i>) of Africa are sometimes included. Both genera are in the banana family, <a href="/wiki/Musaceae" title="Musaceae">Musaceae</a>.
</p><p>Banana plantations are subject to damage by parasitic nematodes and insect pests, and to fungal and bacterial diseases, one of the most serious being Panama disease which is caused by a <i><a href="/wiki/Fusarium" title="Fusarium">Fusarium</a></i> fungus. This and <a href="/wiki/Black_sigatoka" title="Black sigatoka">black sigatoka</a> threaten the production of <a href="/wiki/Cavendish_banana" title="Cavendish banana">Cavendish bananas</a>, the main kind eaten in the Western world, which is a <a href="/wiki/Triploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Triploid">triploid</a> <i>Musa acuminata</i>. Plant breeders are seeking new varieties, but these are difficult to breed given that commercial varieties are seedless. To enable future breeding, banana <a href="/wiki/Germplasm" title="Germplasm">germplasm</a> is conserved in multiple gene banks around the world.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span></h2>
<p>The banana plant is the largest <a href="/wiki/Herbaceous" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbaceous">herbaceous</a> flowering plant.<sup id="cite_ref-PicqINIB00_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PicqINIB00-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> All the above-ground parts of a banana plant grow from a structure called a <a href="/wiki/Corm" title="Corm">corm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds19875–17_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds19875–17-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Plants are normally tall and fairly sturdy with a <a href="/wiki/Tree" title="Tree">treelike</a> appearance, but what appears to be a trunk is actually a <a href="/wiki/Pseudostem" class="mw-redirect" title="Pseudostem">pseudostem</a> composed of multiple leaf-stalks (<a href="/wiki/Petiole_(botany)" title="Petiole (botany)">petioles</a>). Bananas grow in a wide variety of soils, as long as it is at least 60 centimetres (2.0&#160;ft) deep, has good drainage and is not compacted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds1987212_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds1987212-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> They are among the fastest growing of all plants, with daily surface growth rates recorded from 1.4 square metres (15&#160;sq&#160;ft) to 1.6 square metres (17&#160;sq&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The leaves of banana plants are composed of a stalk (<a href="/wiki/Petiole_(botany)" title="Petiole (botany)">petiole</a>) and a blade (<a href="/wiki/Leaf#General_characteristics_of_leaves" title="Leaf">lamina</a>). The base of the petiole widens to form a sheath; the tightly packed sheaths make up the pseudostem, which is all that supports the plant. The edges of the sheath meet when it is first produced, making it tubular. As new growth occurs in the centre of the pseudostem, the edges are forced apart.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds19875–17_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds19875–17-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Cultivated banana plants vary in height depending on the variety and growing conditions. Most are around 5&#160;m (16&#160;ft) tall, with a range from 'Dwarf Cavendish' plants at around 3&#160;m (10&#160;ft) to 'Gros Michel' at 7&#160;m (23&#160;ft) or more.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson200712_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson200712-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Leaves are spirally arranged and may grow 2.7 metres (8.9&#160;ft) long and 60&#160;cm (2.0&#160;ft) wide.<sup id="cite_ref-morton_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morton-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> When a banana plant is mature, the corm stops producing new leaves and begins to form a flower spike or <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">inflorescence</a>. A stem develops which grows up inside the pseudostem, carrying the immature inflorescence until eventually it emerges at the top.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds19875–17_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds19875–17-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Each pseudostem normally produces a single inflorescence, also known as the "banana heart". After fruiting, the pseudostem dies, but offshoots will normally have developed from the base, so that the plant as a whole is <a href="/wiki/Perennial" title="Perennial">perennial</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds1987244–247_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds1987244–247-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The inflorescence contains many petal-like <a href="/wiki/Bract" title="Bract">bracts</a> between rows of flowers. The female flowers (which can develop into fruit) appear in rows further up the stem (closer to the leaves) from the rows of male flowers. The ovary is <a href="/wiki/Ovary_(plants)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ovary (plants)">inferior</a>, meaning that the tiny petals and other flower parts appear at the tip of the ovary.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffice_of_the_Gene_Technology_Regulator2008_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffice_of_the_Gene_Technology_Regulator2008-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The banana fruits develop from the banana heart, in a large hanging cluster called a bunch, made up of around 9 tiers called hands, with up to 20 fruits to a hand. A bunch can weigh 22–65 kilograms (49–143&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The fruit has been described as a "leathery berry".<sup id="cite_ref-Smit77_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smit77-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> There is a protective outer layer (a <a href="/wiki/Peel_(fruit)" title="Peel (fruit)">peel</a> or skin) with numerous long, thin strings (<a href="/wiki/Vascular_bundle" title="Vascular bundle">Vascular bundles</a>), which run lengthwise between the skin and the edible inner portion. The inner part of the common yellow dessert variety can be split lengthwise into three sections that correspond to the inner portions of the three <a href="/wiki/Carpel" class="mw-redirect" title="Carpel">carpels</a> by manually deforming the unopened fruit.<sup id="cite_ref-Wark04_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wark04-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In cultivated varieties, the seeds are diminished nearly to non-existence; their remnants are tiny black specks inside the fruit.<sup id="cite_ref-Simm62_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Simm62-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phylogeny">Phylogeny</span></h3>
<p>A 2011 phylogenomic analysis using nuclear genes indicates the <a href="/wiki/Phylogeny" class="mw-redirect" title="Phylogeny">phylogeny</a> of some representatives of the <a href="/wiki/Musaceae" title="Musaceae">Musaceae</a> family. Major edible kinds of banana are shown in <b>boldface</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-Christelová_Valárik_Hřibová_De_Langhe_2011_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christelová_Valárik_Hřibová_De_Langhe_2011-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>

<dl><dd><sup>‡</sup> <small>Many <a href="/wiki/List_of_banana_cultivars" title="List of banana cultivars">cultivated bananas</a> are hybrids of <i>M. acuminata</i> x <i>M. balbisiana</i> (not shown in tree).<sup id="cite_ref-WCSP_Mp_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCSP_Mp-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup></small></dd></dl>
<p>Work by Li and colleagues in 2024 identifies three subspecies of <i>M. acuminata</i>, namely sspp. <i>banksii</i>, <i>malaccensis</i>, and <i>zebrina</i>, as contributing substantially to the <i>Ban</i>, <i>Dh</i>, and <i>Ze</i> subgenomes of <a href="/wiki/Triploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Triploid">triploid</a> cultivated bananas respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Li_Yu_Cheng_Chang_2024_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Li_Yu_Cheng_Chang_2024-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_banana_cultivars" title="List of banana cultivars">List of banana cultivars</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Nedravazhakola.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Nedravazhakola.jpg/220px-Nedravazhakola.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Nedravazhakola.jpg/330px-Nedravazhakola.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Nedravazhakola.jpg/440px-Nedravazhakola.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a><figcaption><i>Musa</i> 'Nendran' <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivar</a>, grown widely in the Indian state of <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>The genus <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_(genus)" title="Musa (genus)">Musa</a></i> was created by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in 1753.<sup id="cite_ref-WCSP_Musa_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCSP_Musa-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The name may be derived from <a href="/wiki/Antonius_Musa" title="Antonius Musa">Antonius Musa</a>, physician to the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a>, or Linnaeus may have adapted the Arabic word for banana, <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D9%85%D9%88%D8%B2" class="extiw" title="wikt:موز">mauz</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-HyamPank95_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HyamPank95-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The ultimate origin of <i>musa</i> may be in the <a href="/wiki/Trans%E2%80%93New_Guinea_languages" title="Trans–New Guinea languages">Trans–New Guinea languages</a>, which have words similar to "#muku"; from there the name was borrowed into the <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">Austronesian languages</a> and across Asia, accompanying the cultivation of the banana as it was brought to new areas, via the <a href="/wiki/Dravidian_languages" title="Dravidian languages">Dravidian languages</a> of India, into Arabic as a <i><a href="/wiki/Wanderwort" title="Wanderwort">Wanderwort</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The word "banana" is thought to be of West African origin, possibly from the <a href="/wiki/Wolof_language" title="Wolof language">Wolof</a> word <span title="Wolof-language text"><i lang="wo"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/banaana" class="extiw" title="wikt:banaana">banaana</a></i></span>, and passed into <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> via <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a> or <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OEtymD_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OEtymD-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Musa</i> is the type genus in the family <a href="/wiki/Musaceae" title="Musaceae">Musaceae</a>. The <a href="/wiki/APG_III_system" title="APG III system">APG III system</a> assigns Musaceae to the order <a href="/wiki/Zingiberales" title="Zingiberales">Zingiberales</a>, part of the <a href="/wiki/Commelinid" class="mw-redirect" title="Commelinid">commelinid</a> clade of the <a href="/wiki/Monocotyledon" title="Monocotyledon">monocotyledonous</a> flowering plants. Some 70 species of <i>Musa</i> were recognized by the <a href="/wiki/World_Checklist_of_Selected_Plant_Families" title="World Checklist of Selected Plant Families">World Checklist of Selected Plant Families</a> as of January&#160;2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Banana&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>;<sup id="cite_ref-WCSP_Musa_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCSP_Musa-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> several produce edible fruit, while others are cultivated as ornamentals.<sup id="cite_ref-Bail16_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bail16-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The classification of cultivated bananas has long been a problematic issue for taxonomists. Linnaeus originally placed bananas into two species based only on their uses as food: <i>Musa sapientum</i> for dessert bananas and <i>Musa paradisiaca</i> for <a href="/wiki/Plantain_(true)" class="mw-redirect" title="Plantain (true)">plantains</a>. More species names were added, but this approach proved to be inadequate for the number of <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a> in the primary <a href="/wiki/Center_of_diversity" class="mw-redirect" title="Center of diversity">center of diversity</a> of the genus, Southeast Asia. Many of these cultivars were given names that were later discovered to be <a href="/wiki/Synonym_(taxonomy)" title="Synonym (taxonomy)">synonyms</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo2000_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo2000-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a series of papers published from 1947 onwards, Ernest Cheesman showed that Linnaeus's <i>Musa sapientum</i> and <i>Musa paradisiaca</i> were cultivars and descendants of two wild seed-producing species, <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_acuminata" title="Musa acuminata">Musa acuminata</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_balbisiana" title="Musa balbisiana">Musa balbisiana</a></i>, both first described by <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Aloysius_Colla" title="Luigi Aloysius Colla">Luigi Aloysius Colla</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DRC_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DRC-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Cheesman recommended the abolition of Linnaeus's species in favor of reclassifying bananas according to three morphologically distinct groups of cultivars – those primarily exhibiting the botanical characteristics of <i>Musa balbisiana</i>, those primarily exhibiting the botanical characteristics of <i>Musa acuminata</i>, and those with characteristics of both.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo2000_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo2000-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Researchers Norman Simmonds and Ken Shepherd proposed a genome-based nomenclature system in 1955. This system eliminated almost all the difficulties and inconsistencies of the earlier classification of bananas based on assigning scientific names to cultivated varieties. Despite this, the original names are still recognized by some authorities, leading to confusion.<sup id="cite_ref-DRC_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DRC-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Porc02_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porc02-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The accepted <a href="/wiki/Botanical_name" title="Botanical name">scientific names</a> for most groups of cultivated bananas are <i>Musa acuminata</i> <small>Colla</small> and <i>Musa balbisiana</i> <small>Colla</small> for the ancestral species, and <a href="/wiki/Musa_%C3%97_paradisiaca" title="Musa × paradisiaca"><i>Musa</i> × <i>paradisiaca</i></a> <small>L.</small> for the hybrid of the two.<sup id="cite_ref-WCSP_Mp_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCSP_Mp-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Informal_classification">Informal classification</span></h3>
<p>In regions such as North America and Europe, <i>Musa</i> fruits offered for sale can be divided into small sweet "bananas" eaten raw when ripe as a dessert, and large starchy "plantains" or <a href="/wiki/Cooking_banana" title="Cooking banana">cooking bananas</a>, which do not have to be ripe. Linnaeus made this distinction when naming two "species" of <i>Musa</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo20002_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo20002-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Members of the "<a href="/wiki/Plantain_(true)" class="mw-redirect" title="Plantain (true)">plantain subgroup</a>" of banana cultivars, most important as food in West Africa and Latin America, correspond to this description, having long pointed fruit. They are described by Ploetz et al. as "true" plantains, distinct from other cooking bananas.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson200718–19_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson200718–19-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>The cooking bananas of East Africa however belong to a different group, the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Highland_banana" class="mw-redirect" title="East African Highland banana">East African Highland bananas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson200712_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson200712-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Further, small farmers in Colombia grow a much wider range of cultivars than large commercial plantations do,<sup id="cite_ref-GibeDufoGiraSanc09_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GibeDufoGiraSanc09-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> and in Southeast Asia—the center of diversity for bananas, both wild and cultivated—the distinction between "bananas" and "plantains" does not work. Many bananas are used both raw and cooked. There are starchy cooking bananas which are smaller than those eaten raw. The range of colors, sizes and shapes is far wider than in those grown or sold in Africa, Europe or the Americas.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo20002_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo20002-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Southeast Asian languages do not make the distinction between "bananas" and "plantains" that is made in English. Thus both Cavendish dessert bananas and <a href="/wiki/Saba_banana" title="Saba banana">Saba cooking bananas</a> are called <i>pisang</i> in Malaysia and Indonesia, <i>kluai</i> in Thailand and <i>chuối</i> in Vietnam.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo20008–12_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo20008–12-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fe%27i_banana" title="Fe&#39;i banana">Fe'i bananas</a>, grown and eaten in the islands of the Pacific, are derived from a different wild species. Most Fe'i bananas are cooked, but <a href="/wiki/Karat_banana" title="Karat banana">Karat bananas</a>, which are short and squat with bright red skins, are eaten raw.<sup id="cite_ref-Engl03_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Engl03-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestication">Domestication</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Musa_acuminata" title="Musa acuminata">Musa acuminata</a>, <a href="/wiki/Domesticated_plants_and_animals_of_Austronesia" title="Domesticated plants and animals of Austronesia">Domesticated plants and animals of Austronesia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/East_African_Highland_bananas" class="mw-redirect" title="East African Highland bananas">East African Highland bananas</a></div>
<p>The earliest domestication of bananas (<i><a href="/wiki/Musa_(genus)" title="Musa (genus)">Musa</a></i> spp.) was from naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Parthenocarpic" class="mw-redirect" title="Parthenocarpic">parthenocarpic</a> (seedless) individuals of <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_banksii" title="Musa banksii">Musa banksii</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelsonPloetzKepler2006_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelsonPloetzKepler2006-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> These were cultivated by <a href="/wiki/Papuans" class="mw-redirect" title="Papuans">Papuans</a> before the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples" title="Austronesian peoples">Austronesian-speakers</a>. Numerous <a href="/wiki/Phytolith" title="Phytolith">phytoliths</a> of bananas have been recovered from the <a href="/wiki/Kuk_Swamp" title="Kuk Swamp">Kuk Swamp</a> archaeological site and dated to around 10,000 to 6,500 <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Denham2011_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denham2011-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fuller2015_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller2015-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Foraging" title="Foraging">Foraging</a> humans in this area began domestication in the late <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> using <a href="/wiki/Transplanting" title="Transplanting">transplantation</a> and early <a href="/wiki/Tillage" title="Tillage">cultivation</a> methods.<sup id="cite_ref-Roberts-et-al-2017_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-et-al-2017-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Harris-Hillman-1989_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-Hillman-1989-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> By the early to middle of the <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> the process was complete.<sup id="cite_ref-Harris-Hillman-1989_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-Hillman-1989-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Roberts-et-al-2017_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-et-al-2017-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> From New Guinea, cultivated bananas spread westward into <a href="/wiki/Island_Southeast_Asia" class="mw-redirect" title="Island Southeast Asia">Island Southeast Asia</a> through proximity (not migrations). They <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridized</a> with other (possibly independently domesticated) <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> of <i>Musa acuminata</i> as well as <i>M. balbisiana</i> in the Philippines, northern New Guinea, and possibly <a href="/wiki/Halmahera" title="Halmahera">Halmahera</a>. These hybridization events produced the triploid <a href="/wiki/List_of_banana_cultivars" title="List of banana cultivars">cultivars of bananas</a> commonly grown today.<sup id="cite_ref-Denham2011_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denham2011-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spread">Spread</span></h3>
<p>From Island Southeast Asia, bananas became part of the staple domesticated crops of <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples" title="Austronesian peoples">Austronesian peoples</a> and were spread during their voyages and <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_maritime_trade_network" class="mw-redirect" title="Austronesian maritime trade network">ancient maritime trading routes</a> into Oceania, <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indochina" class="mw-redirect" title="Indochina">Indochina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Denham2011_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denham2011-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fuller2015_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller2015-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These ancient introductions resulted in the banana subgroup now known as the <a href="/wiki/True_plantains" title="True plantains">true plantains</a>, which include the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Highland_bananas" class="mw-redirect" title="East African Highland bananas">East African Highland bananas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_plantains" class="mw-redirect" title="Pacific plantains">Pacific plantains</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Iholena_bananas" class="mw-redirect" title="Iholena bananas">Iholena</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maoli-Popo%27ulu_bananas" class="mw-redirect" title="Maoli-Popo&#39;ulu bananas">Maoli-Popo'ulu</a> subgroups). East African Highland bananas originated from banana populations introduced to Madagascar probably from the region between <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>, <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>; while Pacific plantains were introduced to the Pacific Islands from either eastern New Guinea or the <a href="/wiki/Bismarck_Archipelago" title="Bismarck Archipelago">Bismarck Archipelago</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Denham2011_31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denham2011-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Phytolith" title="Phytolith">Phytolith</a> discoveries in Cameroon dating to the first millennium BCE<sup id="cite_ref-Mbid00_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mbid00-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> triggered an as yet unresolved debate about the date of first cultivation in Africa. There is linguistic evidence that bananas were known in East Africa or Madagascar around that time.<sup id="cite_ref-Zell05_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zell05-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The earliest prior evidence indicates that cultivation dates to no earlier than the late 6th century CE.<sup id="cite_ref-Lejj05_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lejj05-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> It is likely, however, that bananas were brought at least to Madagascar if not to the East African coast during the phase of <a href="/wiki/Malagasy_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Malagasy people">Malagasy</a> colonization of the island from South East Asia c. 400 CE.<sup id="cite_ref-Rand09_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rand09-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Glucanase" title="Glucanase">Glucanase</a> and two other proteins specific to bananas were found in <a href="/wiki/Dental_calculus" class="mw-redirect" title="Dental calculus">dental calculus</a> from the early <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> (12th century BCE) <a href="/wiki/Philistines" title="Philistines">Philistines</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tel_Erani" title="Tel Erani">Tel Erani</a> in the southern <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another wave of introductions later spread bananas to other parts of tropical Asia, particularly Indochina and the Indian subcontinent.<sup id="cite_ref-Denham2011_31-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denham2011-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> However, there is evidence that bananas were known to the <a href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Indus Valley civilisation">Indus Valley civilisation</a> from phytoliths recovered from the <a href="/wiki/Kot_Diji" title="Kot Diji">Kot Diji</a> archaeological site in Pakistan. This may indicate very early dispersal of bananas by Austronesian traders by sea from as early as 2000 BCE, or they may have come from local wild <i>Musa</i> species used for fiber or as ornamentals, not food.<sup id="cite_ref-Fuller2015_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller2015-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Southeast Asia remains the region of <a href="/wiki/Center_of_diversity" class="mw-redirect" title="Center of diversity">primary diversity</a> of the banana. Areas of secondary diversity are found in Africa, indicating a long history of banana cultivation there.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson20077_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson20077-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arab_Agricultural_Revolution">Arab Agricultural Revolution</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Arab_Agricultural_Revolution" title="Arab Agricultural Revolution">Arab Agricultural Revolution</a></div>
<p>The banana may have been present in isolated locations elsewhere in the Middle East on the eve of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Spread_of_Islam" title="Spread of Islam">spread of Islam</a> was followed by far-reaching diffusion. There are numerous references to it in Islamic texts (such as poems and <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadiths</a>) beginning in the 9th century. By the 10th century the banana appears in texts from <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a> and Egypt. From there it diffused into North Africa and <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Muslim Iberia</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Agricultural_Revolution" title="Arab Agricultural Revolution">Arab Agricultural Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_1974_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson_1974-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wats83_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wats83-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> An article on banana tree cultivation is included in <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-%27Awwam" title="Ibn al-&#39;Awwam">Ibn al-'Awwam</a>'s 12th-century agricultural work, <i>Kitāb al-Filāḥa</i> (<i>Book on Agriculture</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> During the Middle Ages, bananas from <a href="/wiki/Granada" title="Granada">Granada</a> were considered among the best in the Arab world.<sup id="cite_ref-Wats83_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wats83-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Bananas were certainly grown in the Christian <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Cyprus" title="Kingdom of Cyprus">Kingdom of Cyprus</a> by the late medieval period. Writing in 1458, the Italian traveller and writer <a href="/wiki/Gabriele_Capodilista" title="Gabriele Capodilista">Gabriele Capodilista</a> wrote favourably of the extensive farm produce of the estates at Episkopi, near modern-day <a href="/wiki/Limassol" title="Limassol">Limassol</a>, including the region's banana plantations.<sup id="cite_ref-Jenn92_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jenn92-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Columbian_exchange">Columbian exchange</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Columbian_exchange" title="Columbian exchange">Columbian exchange</a></div>
<p>Bananas were encountered by European explorers during the <a href="/wiki/Magellan_expedition" title="Magellan expedition">Magellan expedition</a> in 1521, in both <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>. Lacking a name for the fruit, the ship's historian <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Pigafetta" title="Antonio Pigafetta">Antonio Pigafetta</a> described them as "figs more than one <a href="/wiki/Palm_(unit)" title="Palm (unit)">palm</a> long."<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nowell_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nowell-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 130, 132">&#58;&#8202;130,&#8202;132&#8202;</span></sup> Bananas were introduced to <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> by Portuguese sailors who brought them from West Africa in the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Botgard_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Botgard-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Southeast Asian banana cultivars, as well as <a href="/wiki/Abaca" class="mw-redirect" title="Abaca">abaca</a> grown for fibers, were introduced to North and Central America by the Spanish from the Philippines, via the <a href="/wiki/Manila_galleons" class="mw-redirect" title="Manila galleons">Manila galleons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Many <a href="/wiki/Musa_(genus)#Species" title="Musa (genus)">wild banana species</a> and cultivars exist in India, China, and Southeast Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-Peed11_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peed11-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-nolines center">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 255px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 250px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Banana_ancestors_(Musa_acuminata_and_Musa_balbisiana)_original_range.png" class="mw-file-description" title="Original native ranges of the ancestors of modern edible bananas. Musa acuminata (green), Musa balbisiana (orange)"><img alt="Original native ranges of the ancestors of modern edible bananas. </ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plantation_cultivation">Plantation cultivation</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_modern_banana_plantations_in_the_Americas" title="History of modern banana plantations in the Americas">History of modern banana plantations in the Americas</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Banana_Plantation_Panabo_City.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Banana_Plantation_Panabo_City.jpg/220px-Banana_Plantation_Panabo_City.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Banana_Plantation_Panabo_City.jpg/330px-Banana_Plantation_Panabo_City.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Banana_Plantation_Panabo_City.jpg/440px-Banana_Plantation_Panabo_City.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a><figcaption>Plantation in the Philippines, 2010</figcaption></figure>
<p>In the 15th and 16th centuries, Portuguese colonists started banana plantations in the Atlantic Islands, Brazil, and western Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Phora_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phora-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> North Americans began consuming bananas on a small scale at very high prices shortly after the Civil War, though it was only in the 1880s that the food became more widespread.<sup id="cite_ref-Koep08_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koep08-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> As late as the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_Era" class="mw-redirect" title="Victorian Era">Victorian Era</a>, bananas were not widely known in Europe, although they were available.<sup id="cite_ref-Phora_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phora-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> 
The earliest modern plantations originated in Jamaica and the related <a href="/wiki/Western_Caribbean_Zone" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Caribbean Zone">Western Caribbean Zone</a>, including most of <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a>. It involved the combination of modern transportation networks of steamships and railroads with the development of refrigeration that allowed more time between harvesting and ripening. North American shippers like <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_Dow_Baker" title="Lorenzo Dow Baker">Lorenzo Dow Baker</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Preston_(businessman)" title="Andrew Preston (businessman)">Andrew Preston</a>, the founders of the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Fruit_Company" title="Boston Fruit Company">Boston Fruit Company</a> started this process in the 1870s, but railroad builders like <a href="/wiki/Minor_C._Keith" class="mw-redirect" title="Minor C. Keith">Minor C. Keith</a> participated, culminating in the multi-national giant corporations like <a href="/wiki/Chiquita_Brands_International" class="mw-redirect" title="Chiquita Brands International">Chiquita</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dole_Food_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Dole Food Company">Dole</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Koep08_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koep08-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> These companies were <a href="/wiki/Monopoly" title="Monopoly">monopolistic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vertically_integrated" class="mw-redirect" title="Vertically integrated">vertically integrated</a> (controlling growing, processing, shipping and marketing) and usually used political manipulation to build <a href="/wiki/Enclave_economy" title="Enclave economy">enclave economies</a> (internally self-sufficient, virtually tax exempt, and export-oriented, contributing little to the host economy). Their political maneuvers, which gave rise to the term <a href="/wiki/Banana_republic" title="Banana republic">banana republic</a> for states such as Honduras and Guatemala, included working with local elites and their rivalries to influence politics or playing the international interests of the United States, especially during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, to keep the political climate favorable to their interests.<sup id="cite_ref-NZH_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NZH-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In the modern United States, <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a> is by far the largest banana producer, followed by <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peasant_cultivation">Peasant cultivation</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Farm_Workers.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Farm_Workers.jpg/220px-Farm_Workers.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Farm_Workers.jpg/330px-Farm_Workers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Farm_Workers.jpg/440px-Farm_Workers.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1696" data-file-height="954" /></a><figcaption>Small-scale banana production, Liberia, 2013</figcaption></figure>
<p>The vast majority of the world's bananas are cultivated for family consumption or for sale on local markets. India is the world leader in this sort of production, but many other Asian and African countries where climate and soil conditions allow cultivation also host large populations of banana growers who sell at least some of their crop.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffice_of_the_Gene_Technology_Regulator20087–8_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffice_of_the_Gene_Technology_Regulator20087–8-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/History_of_peasant_banana_production_in_the_Americas" title="History of peasant banana production in the Americas">Peasants with smallholdings of 1 to 2 acres in the Caribbean produce bananas</a> for the world market, often alongside other crops.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_cultivation">Modern cultivation</span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_banana_cultivars" title="List of banana cultivars">List of banana cultivars</a></div>
<p>Bananas are propagated <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">asexually</a> from offshoots. The plant is allowed to produce two shoots at a time; a larger one for immediate fruiting and a smaller "sucker" or "follower" to produce fruit in 6–8 months.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds1987244–247_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds1987244–247-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> As a non-seasonal crop, bananas are available fresh year-round.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> They are grown in some 135 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cavendish">Cavendish</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cavendish_banana" title="Cavendish banana">Cavendish banana</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Bananas.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Grocery store photo of several bunches of bananas" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Bananas.jpg/220px-Bananas.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Bananas.jpg/330px-Bananas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Bananas.jpg/440px-Bananas.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a><figcaption>Cultivars in the <a href="/wiki/Cavendish_bananas" class="mw-redirect" title="Cavendish bananas">Cavendish</a> group dominate the world market.</figcaption></figure>
<p>In global commerce in 2009, by far the most important cultivars belonged to the triploid <i>Musa acuminata</i> <a href="/wiki/List_of_banana_cultivars#AAA_Group" title="List of banana cultivars">AAA group</a> of Cavendish group bananas.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> It is unclear if any existing cultivar can replace Cavendish bananas, so various <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridisation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a> programs are attempting to create a disease-resistant, mass-market banana. One such strain that has emerged is the Taiwanese Cavendish or Formosana.<sup id="cite_ref-Gittleson_2018_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gittleson_2018-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ripening">Ripening</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Overripe_banana_20211221_152406.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Overripe_banana_20211221_152406.jpg/220px-Overripe_banana_20211221_152406.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="117" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Overripe_banana_20211221_152406.jpg/330px-Overripe_banana_20211221_152406.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Overripe_banana_20211221_152406.jpg/440px-Overripe_banana_20211221_152406.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5344" data-file-height="2832" /></a><figcaption><i><a href="/wiki/Ralstonia_solanacearum" title="Ralstonia solanacearum">Ralstonia solanacearum</a></i> on an overripe banana</figcaption></figure>
<p>Export bananas are picked green, and ripened in special rooms upon arrival in the destination country. These rooms are air-tight and filled with <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_as_a_plant_hormone" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethylene as a plant hormone">ethylene gas</a> to induce ripening. This mimics the normal production of this gas as a ripening hormone.<sup id="cite_ref-plantphys_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plantphys-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-newton_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newton-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Ethylene stimulates the formation of <a href="/wiki/Amylase" title="Amylase">amylase</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> that breaks down starch into sugar, influencing the taste. Ethylene signals the production of <a href="/wiki/Pectinase" title="Pectinase">pectinase</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> which breaks down the <a href="/wiki/Pectin" title="Pectin">pectin</a> between the cells of the banana, causing the banana to soften as it ripens.<sup id="cite_ref-plantphys_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plantphys-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-newton_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newton-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The vivid yellow color consumers normally associate with supermarket bananas is caused by ripening around 18&#160;°C (64&#160;°F), and does not occur in Cavendish bananas ripened in tropical temperatures (over 27&#160;°C (81&#160;°F)).<sup id="cite_ref-Ding07_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ding07-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Storage_and_transport">Storage and transport</span></h3>
<p>Bananas are transported over long distances from the tropics to world markets.<sup id="cite_ref-FAO-2003_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO-2003-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> To obtain maximum shelf life, harvest comes before the fruit is mature. The fruit requires careful handling, rapid transport to ports, cooling, and refrigerated shipping. The goal is to prevent the bananas from producing their natural ripening agent, ethylene. This technology allows storage and transport for 3–4 weeks at 13&#160;°C (55&#160;°F). On arrival, bananas are held at about 17&#160;°C (63&#160;°F) and treated with a low concentration of ethylene. After a few days, the fruit begins to ripen and is distributed for final sale. Ripe bananas can be held for a few days at home. If bananas are too green, they can be put in a brown paper bag with an apple or tomato overnight to speed up the ripening process.<sup id="cite_ref-ChiquitaRipen_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChiquitaRipen-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sustainability">Sustainability</span></h3>
<p>The excessive use of fertilizers contributes greatly to <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a> in streams and lakes, and harms aquatic life after algal blooms deprive fish of oxygen. It has been theorized that destruction of 60% of coral reefs along the coasts of Costa Rica is partially from sediments from banana plantations. Another issue is the deforestation associated with expanding banana production. As monocultures rapidly deplete soil nutrients plantations expand to areas with rich soils and cut down forests, which also affects soil erosion and degradation, and increases frequency of flooding. The <a href="/wiki/World_Wildlife_Fund" class="mw-redirect" title="World Wildlife Fund">World Wildlife Fund</a> stated that banana production produced more waste than any other agricultural sector, with discarded banana plants, bags used to cover the bananas, strings to tie them, and containers for transport.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohen2009_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2009-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sustainability_standards_and_certification" title="Sustainability standards and certification">Voluntary sustainability standards</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Rainforest_Alliance" title="Rainforest Alliance">Rainforest Alliance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fairtrade_certification" class="mw-redirect" title="Fairtrade certification">Fairtrade</a> are being used to address some of these issues. Bananas production certified in this way grew rapidly at the start of the 21st century to represent 36% of banana exports by 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mutation_breeding" title="Mutation breeding">Mutation breeding</a> can be used in this crop. <a href="/wiki/Aneuploidy" title="Aneuploidy">Aneuploidy</a> is a source of significant variation in <a href="/wiki/Allotriploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Allotriploid">allotriploid</a> varieties. For one example, it can be a source of <a href="/wiki/Panama_disease#Tropical_Race_4/TR4" title="Panama disease">TR4 resistance</a>. <a href="/wiki/Lab_protocol" class="mw-redirect" title="Lab protocol">Lab protocols</a> have been devised to screen for such aberrations and for possible resulting disease resistances.<sup id="cite_ref-Efficient-Screening-Mutants_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Efficient-Screening-Mutants-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Crop_wild_relative" title="Crop wild relative">Wild <i>Musa</i> spp.</a> provide useful resistance genetics, and are vital to breeding for TR4 resistance, as shown in <a href="/wiki/Introgressed" class="mw-redirect" title="Introgressed">introgressed</a> resistance from wild relatives.<sup id="cite_ref-Current-Update_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Current-Update-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Honduran_Foundation_for_Agricultural_Research" class="mw-redirect" title="Honduran Foundation for Agricultural Research">Honduran Foundation for Agricultural Research</a> attempted to exploit the rare cases of seed production to create disease-resistant varieties by conventional breeding; 30,000 commercial banana plants were hand-pollinated with pollen from wild fertile Asian fruit, producing 400 tonnes, which contained about fifteen seeds, of which four or five germinated. Further breeding with wild bananas yielded a new seedless variety resistant to both black Sigatoka and Panama disease.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
