<tr>
<td colspan="4"><small>Source: <a href="/wiki/FAOSTAT" class="mw-redirect" title="FAOSTAT">FAOSTAT</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a><sup id="cite_ref-FAOSTAT2022_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAOSTAT2022-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Note: Some countries distinguish between bananas and plantains, but four of the top six producers do not, thus necessitating comparisons using the total for bananas and plantains combined.</small>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Bananas are exported in larger volume and to a larger value than any other fruit.<sup id="cite_ref-Gittleson_2018_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gittleson_2018-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> In 2022, world production of bananas and plantains combined was 179 million tonnes, led by India and China with a combined total of 26% of global production. Other major producers were Uganda, Indonesia, the Philippines, Nigeria and Ecuador.<sup id="cite_ref-FAOSTAT2022_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAOSTAT2022-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> As reported for 2013, total world exports were 20 million tonnes of bananas and 859,000 tonnes of plantains.<sup id="cite_ref-faostat13exp_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faostat13exp-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Ecuador and the Philippines were the leading exporters with 5.4 and 3.3 million tonnes, respectively, and the Dominican Republic was the leading exporter of plantains with 210,350 tonnes.<sup id="cite_ref-faostat13exp_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faostat13exp-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Developing_countries">Developing countries</span></h3>
<p>Bananas and plantains are a major staple <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">food crop</a> for millions of people in <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a>. In many tropical countries, the main cultivars produce green (unripe) bananas used for <a href="/wiki/Cooking" title="Cooking">cooking</a>. Most producers are small-scale <a href="/wiki/Farmer" title="Farmer">farmers</a> either for home consumption or local markets. Because bananas and plantains produce fruit year-round, they provide a valuable food source during the <i>hunger season</i> between harvests of other crops. Bananas and plantains are thus important for global <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dhon_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dhon-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pests">Pests</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nematodes">Nematodes</span></h3>
<p>Banana roots are subject to damage from multiple species of parasitic <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematodes</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Radopholus_similis" title="Radopholus similis">Radopholus similis</a></i> causes nematode root rot, the most serious nematode disease of bananas in economic terms.<sup id="cite_ref-Sekora_Crow_2012_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sekora_Crow_2012-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Root-knot is the result of infection by species of <i><a href="/wiki/Meloidogyne" class="mw-redirect" title="Meloidogyne">Meloidogyne</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> while root-lesion is caused by species of <i><a href="/wiki/Pratylenchus" title="Pratylenchus">Pratylenchus</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Nyang’au_Atandi_Cortada_Nchore_2021_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nyang’au_Atandi_Cortada_Nchore_2021-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> and spiral nematode root damage is the result of infection by <i><a href="/wiki/Helicotylenchus" title="Helicotylenchus">Helicotylenchus</a></i> species.<sup id="cite_ref-Zuckerman_1963_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zuckerman_1963-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-center" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Radopholus_similis.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Radopholus_similis.jpg/220px-Radopholus_similis.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Radopholus_similis.jpg/330px-Radopholus_similis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Radopholus_similis.jpg/440px-Radopholus_similis.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2901" data-file-height="1933" /></a><figcaption><i><a href="/wiki/Radopholus_similis" title="Radopholus similis">Radopholus similis</a></i> inside banana root, causing nematode root rot</figcaption></figure>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Insects">Insects</span></h3>
<p>Among the main insect pests of banana cultivation are two beetles that cause substantial economic losses, the banana borer <i><a href="/wiki/Cosmopolites_sordidus" title="Cosmopolites sordidus">Cosmopolites sordidus</a></i> and the banana stem weevil <i><a href="/wiki/Odoiporus_longicollis" title="Odoiporus longicollis">Odoiporus longicollis</a></i>. Other significant pests include <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">aphids</a> and scarring beetles.<sup id="cite_ref-Padmanaban_2018_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Padmanaban_2018-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-center" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Cosmopolites_sordidus.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Cosmopolites_sordidus.jpg/220px-Cosmopolites_sordidus.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="213" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Cosmopolites_sordidus.jpg/330px-Cosmopolites_sordidus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Cosmopolites_sordidus.jpg/440px-Cosmopolites_sordidus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="525" data-file-height="508" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Cosmopolites_sordidus" title="Cosmopolites sordidus">banana borer</a> is a destructive pest that tunnels inside the plant.<sup id="cite_ref-Padmanaban_2018_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Padmanaban_2018-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diseases">Diseases</span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_banana_and_plantain_diseases" title="List of banana and plantain diseases">List of banana and plantain diseases</a></div>
<p>Although in no danger of outright extinction, bananas of the Cavendish group, which dominate the global market, are under threat. Its predecessor '<a href="/wiki/Gros_Michel" class="mw-redirect" title="Gros Michel">Gros Michel</a>', discovered in the 1820s, was similarly dominant but had to be replaced after widespread infections of Panama Disease. <a href="/wiki/Monocropping" title="Monocropping">Monocropping</a> of Cavendish similarly leaves it susceptible to disease and so threatens both commercial cultivation and small-scale subsistence farming.<sup id="cite_ref-NS060613_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NS060613-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mont03_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mont03-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Within the data gathered from the genes of hundreds of bananas, the botanist Julie Sardos has found several wild banana ancestors currently unknown to scientists, whose genes could provide a means of defense against banana crop diseases.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some commentators have remarked that those variants which could replace what much of the world considers a "typical banana" are so different that most people would not consider them the same fruit, and blame the decline of the banana on <a href="/wiki/Mendelian_inheritance" title="Mendelian inheritance">monogenetic</a> cultivation driven by short-term commercial motives.<sup id="cite_ref-NZH_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NZH-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Overall, <a href="/wiki/Fungal_banana_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Fungal banana disease">fungal diseases</a> are disproportionately important to <a href="/wiki/Small_island_developing_states" class="mw-redirect" title="Small island developing states">small island developing states</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas-et-al-2020_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-et-al-2020-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Panama_disease">Panama disease</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Panama_disease_of_banana_-_vascular_decoloration_on_pseudostem.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="A banana tree cut horizontally to show the fungus development in the interior of the tree" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Panama_disease_of_banana_-_vascular_decoloration_on_pseudostem.jpg/220px-Panama_disease_of_banana_-_vascular_decoloration_on_pseudostem.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Panama_disease_of_banana_-_vascular_decoloration_on_pseudostem.jpg/330px-Panama_disease_of_banana_-_vascular_decoloration_on_pseudostem.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Panama_disease_of_banana_-_vascular_decoloration_on_pseudostem.jpg/440px-Panama_disease_of_banana_-_vascular_decoloration_on_pseudostem.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="540" /></a><figcaption>Panama disease <i><a href="/wiki/Fusarium" title="Fusarium">Fusarium</a></i> fungus climbing up through the banana stem</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Panama_disease" title="Panama disease">Panama disease</a> is caused by a <i><a href="/wiki/Fusarium" title="Fusarium">Fusarium</a></i> soil <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungus</a>, which enters the plants through the roots and travels with water into the trunk and leaves, producing <a href="/wiki/Gel" title="Gel">gels</a> and gums that cut off the flow of water and nutrients, causing the plant to <a href="/wiki/Wilting" title="Wilting">wilt</a>, and exposing the rest of the plant to lethal amounts of sunlight. Prior to 1960, almost all commercial banana production centered on the Gros Michel cultivar, which was highly susceptible.<sup id="cite_ref-Bark08_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bark08-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Cavendish was chosen as the replacement for Gros Michel because, among resistant cultivars, it produces the highest quality <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a>. However, more care is required for shipping the Cavendish,<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> and its quality compared to Gros Michel is debated.<sup id="cite_ref-Lessard_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lessard-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fusarium_wilt_TR4">Fusarium wilt TR4</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tropical_Race_4" class="mw-redirect" title="Tropical Race 4">Fusarium wilt TR4</a>, a reinvigorated strain of Panama disease, was discovered in 1993. This virulent form of Fusarium wilt has destroyed Cavendish plantations in several southeast Asian countries and spread to Australia and India.<sup id="cite_ref-karp_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-karp-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> As the soil-based fungi can easily be carried on boots, clothing, or tools, the wilt spread to the Americas despite years of preventive efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-karp_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-karp-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Without genetic diversity, Cavendish is highly susceptible to TR4, and the disease endangers its commercial production worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-IBS_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IBS-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The only known defense to TR4 is <a href="/wiki/Plant_disease_resistance" title="Plant disease resistance">genetic resistance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-karp_87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-karp-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> This is conferred either by <a href="/wiki/RGA2" class="mw-redirect" title="RGA2">RGA2</a>, a gene isolated from a TR4-resistant <a href="/wiki/Diploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Diploid">diploid</a> banana, or by the <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematode</a>-derived Ced9.<sup id="cite_ref-Dale-et-al-2017_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dale-et-al-2017-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ISAAA-Dale-2021_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISAAA-Dale-2021-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> This may be achieved by <a href="/wiki/Genetic_modification" class="mw-redirect" title="Genetic modification">genetic modification</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dale-et-al-2017_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dale-et-al-2017-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ISAAA-Dale-2021_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISAAA-Dale-2021-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Experts state the need to enrich banana <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> by producing diverse new banana varieties, not just having a focus on the Cavendish.<sup id="cite_ref-karp_87-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-karp-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Black_sigatoka">Black sigatoka</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Black_Sigatoka_(detail).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Black_Sigatoka_%28detail%29.jpg/220px-Black_Sigatoka_%28detail%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="140" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Black_Sigatoka_%28detail%29.jpg/330px-Black_Sigatoka_%28detail%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Black_Sigatoka_%28detail%29.jpg/440px-Black_Sigatoka_%28detail%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="976" /></a><figcaption>Leaf infected with <a href="/wiki/Black_sigatoka" title="Black sigatoka">black sigatoka</a></figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Black_sigatoka" title="Black sigatoka">Black sigatoka</a> is a fungal leaf spot disease first observed in Fiji in 1963 or 1964. It is caused by the <a href="/wiki/Ascomycete" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascomycete">ascomycete</a> <i>Mycosphaerella fijiensis</i>. The disease, also called black leaf streak, has spread to banana plantations throughout the tropics from infected banana leaves used as packing material. It affects all main cultivars of bananas and plantains (including the Cavendish cultivars<sup id="cite_ref-Holmes_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>), impeding <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> by blackening parts of the leaves, eventually killing the entire leaf. Starved for energy, fruit production falls by 50% or more, and the bananas that do grow <a href="/wiki/Ripening" title="Ripening">ripen</a> prematurely, making them unsuitable for export. The fungus has shown ever-increasing resistance to treatment, with the current expense for treating 1 hectare (2.5 acres) exceeding US$1,000 per year; spraying with fungicides may be required as often as 50 times a year. In addition to the expense, there is the question of how long intensive spraying can be environmentally justified.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JGI_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JGI-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Banana_bunchy_top_virus">Banana bunchy top virus</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Banana_aphid_colony.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Infected Banana Plant" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Banana_aphid_colony.jpg/220px-Banana_aphid_colony.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Banana_aphid_colony.jpg/330px-Banana_aphid_colony.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Banana_aphid_colony.jpg/440px-Banana_aphid_colony.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a><figcaption>Colony of banana aphids (<i><a href="/wiki/Pentalonia_nigronervosa" title="Pentalonia nigronervosa">Pentalonia nigronervosa</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Disease_vector" title="Disease vector">vector</a> of <a href="/wiki/Banana_bunchy_top_virus" title="Banana bunchy top virus">banana bunchy top virus</a></figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Banana_bunchy_top_virus" title="Banana bunchy top virus">Banana bunchy top virus</a> (BBTV) is a plant virus of the genus <i>Babuvirus</i>, family <i>Nanonviridae</i> affecting <i>Musa</i> spp. (including banana, abaca, plantain and ornamental bananas) and <i>Ensete</i> spp. in the family <i>Musaceae</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Banana bunchy top disease (BBTD) symptoms include dark green streaks of variable length in leaf veins, midribs and petioles. Leaves become short and stunted as the disease progresses, becoming 'bunched' at the apex of the plant. Infected plants may produce no fruit or the fruit bunch may not emerge from the pseudostem.<sup id="cite_ref-techguide_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-techguide-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The virus is transmitted by the banana aphid <i><a href="/wiki/Pentalonia_nigronervosa" title="Pentalonia nigronervosa">Pentalonia nigronervosa</a></i> and is widespread in SE Asia, Asia, the Philippines, Taiwan, Oceania and parts of Africa. There is no cure for BBTD, but it can be effectively controlled by the eradication of diseased plants and the use of virus-free planting material.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> No resistant cultivars have been found, but varietal differences in susceptibility have been reported. The commercially important Cavendish subgroup is severely affected.<sup id="cite_ref-techguide_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-techguide-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Banana_bacterial_wilt">Banana bacterial wilt</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Banana_Xanthomonas_wilt" title="Banana Xanthomonas wilt">Banana bacterial wilt</a> (BBW) is a bacterial disease caused by <i><a href="/wiki/Xanthomonas_campestris" title="Xanthomonas campestris">Xanthomonas campestris</a></i> pv. <i>musacearum</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tush04_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tush04-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> First identified on a close relative of bananas, <i><a href="/wiki/Ensete_ventricosum" title="Ensete ventricosum">Ensete ventricosum</a></i>, in Ethiopia in the 1960s,<sup id="cite_ref-Brad68_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brad68-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> BBW occurred in Uganda in 2001 affecting all banana cultivars. Since then BBW has been diagnosed in Central and East Africa including the banana growing regions of Rwanda, the Democratic Republic of the Congo, Tanzania, Kenya, Burundi, and Uganda.<sup id="cite_ref-Mwan07_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mwan07-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_of_genetic_diversity">Conservation of genetic diversity</span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:ITC_cold_room.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/ITC_cold_room.jpg/220px-ITC_cold_room.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/ITC_cold_room.jpg/330px-ITC_cold_room.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/ITC_cold_room.jpg/440px-ITC_cold_room.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5067" data-file-height="3426" /></a><figcaption>The cold storage room for the banana collection at <a href="/wiki/Bioversity_International" title="Bioversity International">Bioversity International</a>'s Musa Germplasm Transit Centre</figcaption></figure>
<p>Given the narrow range of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a> present in bananas and the many threats via <a href="/wiki/Biotic_component" class="mw-redirect" title="Biotic component">biotic</a> (pests and diseases) and <a href="/wiki/Abiotic_component" title="Abiotic component">abiotic</a> threats (such as <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a>) stress, <a href="/wiki/Conservation_biology" title="Conservation biology">conservation</a> of the full spectrum of banana <a href="/wiki/Plant_genetic_resources" title="Plant genetic resources">genetic resources</a> is ongoing.<sup id="cite_ref-genebank_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-genebank-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> In 2024, the economist Pascal Liu of the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">FAO</a> described the impact of <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Global warming">global warming</a> as an "enormous threat" to the world supply of bananas.<sup id="cite_ref-McGrath_2014_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGrath_2014-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Banana <a href="/wiki/Germplasm" title="Germplasm">germplasm</a> is conserved in many national and regional <a href="/wiki/Gene_banks" class="mw-redirect" title="Gene banks">gene banks</a>, and at the world's largest banana collection, the International <i>Musa</i> Germplasm Transit Centre, managed by <a href="/wiki/Bioversity_International" title="Bioversity International">Bioversity International</a> and hosted at <a href="/wiki/KU_Leuven" title="KU Leuven">KU Leuven</a> in Belgium.<sup id="cite_ref-musagermplasm_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-musagermplasm-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> <i>Musa</i> cultivars are usually seedless, and options for their long-term conservation are constrained by the vegetative nature of the plant's reproductive system. Consequently, they are conserved by three main methods: <i><a href="/wiki/In_vivo" title="In vivo">in vivo</a></i> (planted in field collections), <i><a href="/wiki/In_vitro" title="In vitro">in vitro</a></i> (as plantlets in test tubes within a controlled environment), and by <a href="/wiki/Cryopreservation" title="Cryopreservation">cryopreservation</a> (<a href="/wiki/Meristem" title="Meristem">meristems</a> conserved in <a href="/wiki/Liquid_nitrogen" title="Liquid nitrogen">liquid nitrogen</a> at −196&#160;°C).<sup id="cite_ref-genebank_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-genebank-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Genes from wild banana species are conserved as <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and as cryopreserved <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a><sup id="cite_ref-genebank_100-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-genebank-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> and banana seeds from wild species are also conserved, although less commonly, as they are difficult to regenerate. In addition, bananas and their <a href="/wiki/Crop_wild_relatives" class="mw-redirect" title="Crop wild relatives">crop wild relatives</a> are conserved <i><a href="/wiki/In_situ" title="In situ">in situ</a></i> (in wild natural habitats where they evolved and continue to do so). Diversity is also conserved in farmers' fields where continuous cultivation, adaptation and improvement of cultivars is often carried out by small-scale farmers growing traditional local cultivars.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition">Nutrition</span></h2>

biology">Potassium</a></th><td class="infobox-data" style="vertical-align:middle;padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;"><div style="float: right;">12%</div> 358 mg</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" </td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1218072481"></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;font-weight:normal;padding-right:0.25em;"><b style="margin-left:-0.65em">Other constituents</b></th><td class="infobox-data" style="vertical-align:middle;padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;"><b>Quantity</b></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;font-weight:normal;padding-right:0.25em;">Water</th><td class="infobox-data" style="vertical-align:middle;padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;">74.91 g</td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data"><hr /><div class="wrap" style="padding:0.3em;line-height:1.2em;"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160101232525/http://ndb.nal.usda.gov/ndb/search/list?qlookup=09040&amp;format=Full">Link to USDA Database entry</a>
 values are for edible portion</div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-below wrap" style="background:#e0e0e0;padding:0.3em;line-height:1.5em;font-weight:normal;"><sup>†</sup>Percentages estimated using <a href="/wiki/Reference_Daily_Intake#Daily_Values" title="Reference Daily Intake">US&#160;recommendations</a> for adults,<sup id="cite_ref-FDADailyValues_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FDADailyValues-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> except for potassium, which is estimated based on expert recommendation from <a href="/wiki/National_Academies_of_Sciences,_Engineering,_and_Medicine" title="National Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine">the National Academies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NationalAcademiesPotassium_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NationalAcademiesPotassium-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A raw banana (not including the peel) is 75% water, 23% <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, 1% <a href="/wiki/Protein_(nutrient)" title="Protein (nutrient)">protein</a>, and contains negligible <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a>. A reference amount of 100 grams (3.5&#160;oz) supplies 89 <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">calories</a>, 31% of the <a href="/wiki/Daily_Value" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily Value">Daily Value</a> of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B6" title="Vitamin B6">vitamin B<sub>6</sub></a>, and moderate amounts of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manganese_in_biology" title="Manganese in biology">manganese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium_in_biology" title="Potassium in biology">potassium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a>, with no other <a href="/wiki/Micronutrient" title="Micronutrient">micronutrients</a> in significant content (table).
</p><p>Although bananas are commonly thought to contain exceptional potassium content,<sup id="cite_ref-edwards_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-edwards-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> their actual potassium content is not high per typical food serving, having only 12% of the Daily Value for potassium (considered a moderate level of the DV; table). The potassium-content ranking for bananas among fruits, vegetables, legumes, and many other foods is relatively medium.<sup id="cite_ref-rank_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rank-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-eatright_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eatright-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Individuals with a <a href="/wiki/Latex_allergy" title="Latex allergy">latex allergy</a> may experience a reaction to bananas.<sup id="cite_ref-Tayl04_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tayl04-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culinary">Culinary</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fruit">Fruit</span></h4>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1217611005">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9;display:flow-root}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1;min-width:0}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-right plainlinks sistersitebox"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1126788409">.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul{line-height:inherit;list-style:none;margin:0;padding:0}.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol li,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul li{margin-bottom:0}</style>
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/40px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/60px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/80px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="400" /></span></span></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist">Wikibooks <a href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Cookbook" class="extiw" title="wikibooks:Cookbook">Cookbook</a> has a recipe/module on
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409"><div class="plainlist" style="margin-left: 1.6em;">
<ul><li><i><b><a href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Cookbook:Banana" class="extiw" title="wikibooks:Cookbook:Banana"> Banana</a></b></i></li></ul>
</div></div></div>
</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cooking_plantain" class="mw-redirect" title="Cooking plantain">Cooking plantain</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_banana_dishes" title="List of banana dishes">List of banana dishes</a></div>
<p>Bananas are a staple <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> for many tropical populations. Depending upon cultivar and ripeness, the flesh can vary in taste from starchy to sweet, and texture from firm to mushy. Both the skin and inner part can be eaten raw or cooked. The primary component of the aroma of fresh bananas is <a href="/wiki/Isoamyl_acetate" title="Isoamyl acetate">isoamyl acetate</a> (also known as <i>banana oil</i>), which, along with several other compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Butyl_acetate" title="Butyl acetate">butyl acetate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isobutyl_acetate" title="Isobutyl acetate">isobutyl acetate</a>, is a significant contributor to banana flavor.<sup id="cite_ref-Mui02_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mui02-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Plantains are eaten cooked, such as made into fritters.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pisang_goreng" class="mw-redirect" title="Pisang goreng">Pisang goreng</a>, bananas fried with batter is a popular street food in Southeast Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-Street_Food_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Street_Food-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Bananas feature in <a href="/wiki/Philippine_cuisine" class="mw-redirect" title="Philippine cuisine">Philippine cuisine</a>, with desserts like <i><a href="/wiki/Maruya_(Filipino_cuisine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Maruya (Filipino cuisine)">maruya</a></i> banana fritters.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Bananas can be made into fruit preserves.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Banana_chips" class="mw-redirect" title="Banana chips">Banana chips</a> are a snack produced from sliced and fried bananas, such as in <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hindu130413_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hindu130413-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Dried bananas are ground to make <a href="/wiki/Banana_flour" title="Banana flour">banana flour</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-business-goes-bananas_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-business-goes-bananas-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>

		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:YosriPengatPisang.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Banana in sweet gravy, known as pengat pisang in Malaysia"><img alt="Banana in sweet gravy, known as pengat pisang in Malaysia" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/YosriPengatPisang.jpg/300px-YosriPengatPisang.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/YosriPengatPisang.jpg/451px-YosriPengatPisang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/YosriPengatPisang.jpg/600px-YosriPengatPisang.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a></span></div>

		</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flowers">Flowers</span></h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1217611005"><div class="side-box side-box-right plainlinks sistersitebox"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409">
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/40px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/60px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/80px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="400" /></span></span></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist">Wikibooks <a href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Cookbook" class="extiw" title="wikibooks:Cookbook">Cookbook</a> has a recipe/module on
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409"><div class="plainlist" style="margin-left: 1.6em;">
<ul><li><i><b><a href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Cookbook:Banana_Blossom" class="extiw" title="wikibooks:Cookbook:Banana Blossom"> Banana Blossom</a></b></i></li></ul>
</div></div></div>
</div>
<p>Banana flowers (also called "banana hearts" or "banana blossoms") are used as a <a href="/wiki/Vegetable" title="Vegetable">vegetable</a><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/South_Asian_cuisine" title="South Asian cuisine">South Asian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asian_cuisine" class="mw-redirect" title="Southeast Asian cuisine">Southeast Asian cuisine</a>. The flavor resembles that of <a href="/wiki/Artichoke" title="Artichoke">artichoke</a>. As with artichokes, both the fleshy part of the bracts and the heart are edible.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 226px">

		</li>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Leaf">Leaf</span></h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Banana_leaf" title="Banana leaf">Banana leaf</a></div>
<p>Banana leaves are large, flexible, and waterproof. While generally too tough to actually be eaten, they are often used as ecologically friendly disposable food containers or as "plates" in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> and several <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asian</a> countries.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_cuisine" title="Indonesian cuisine">Indonesian cuisine</a>, banana leaf is employed in cooking methods like <a href="/wiki/Pepes" title="Pepes">pepes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Botok" title="Botok">botok</a>; banana leaf packages containing food ingredients and spices are cooked in steam or in boiled water, or are grilled on charcoal. Certain types of tamales are wrapped in banana leaves instead of corn husks.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When used so for steaming or grilling, the banana leaves protect the food ingredients from burning and add a subtle sweet flavor.<sup id="cite_ref-morton_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morton-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/South_India" title="South India">South India</a>, it is customary to serve traditional food on a banana leaf.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a> (India), dried banana leaves are used as to pack food and to make cups to hold liquid food items.<sup id="cite_ref-Kora_2019_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kora_2019-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Chicken_satay_on_banana_leaf_in_Java.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Banana leaf as disposable plate for chicken satay in Java"><img alt="Banana leaf as disposable plate for chicken satay in Java" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Chicken_satay_on_banana_leaf_in_Java.jpg/300px-Chicken_satay_on_banana_leaf_in_Java.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Chicken_satay_on_banana_leaf_in_Java.jpg/451px-Chicken_satay_on_banana_leaf_in_Java.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Chicken_satay_on_banana_leaf_in_Java.jpg/600px-Chicken_satay_on_banana_leaf_in_Java.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1984" data-file-height="1488" /></a></span></div>

		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px">

		</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trunk">Trunk</span></h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Banana_pith" title="Banana pith">Banana pith</a></div>
<p>The tender core of the banana plant's trunk is also used in <a href="/wiki/South_Asian_cuisine" title="South Asian cuisine">South Asian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asian_cuisine" class="mw-redirect" title="Southeast Asian cuisine">Southeast Asian cuisine</a>. Examples include the Burmese dish <i><a href="/wiki/Mohinga" title="Mohinga">mohinga</a></i>, and the <a href="/wiki/Filipino_cuisine" title="Filipino cuisine">Filipino</a> dishes <i><a href="/wiki/Inubaran" title="Inubaran">inubaran</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Kadyos,_manok,_kag_ubad" title="Kadyos, manok, kag ubad">kadyos, manok, kag ubad</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Polistico_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polistico-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Polistico2_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polistico2-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 152px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Kaeng_yuak.JPG" class="mw-file-description" title="Kaeng yuak, a northern Thai curry of the core of the banana plant"><img alt="Kaeng yuak, a northern Thai curry of the core of the banana plant" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Kaeng_yuak.JPG/225px-Kaeng_yuak.JPG" decoding="async" width="150" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Kaeng_yuak.JPG/338px-Kaeng_yuak.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Kaeng_yuak.JPG/450px-Kaeng_yuak.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1918" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext"><i>Kaeng yuak</i>, a northern <a href="/wiki/Thai_curry" title="Thai curry">Thai curry</a> of the core of the banana plant</div>
		</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paper_and_textiles">Paper and textiles</span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Manila_hemp" class="mw-redirect" title="Manila hemp">Manila hemp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Banana_paper" title="Banana paper">Banana paper</a></div>
<p>Banana fiber harvested from the pseudostems and leaves has been used for <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">textiles</a> in Asia since at least the 13th century. Both fruit-bearing and fibrous banana species have been used.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> In the Japanese system <a href="/wiki/Kij%C5%8Dka-bash%C5%8Dfu" title="Kijōka-bashōfu">Kijōka-bashōfu</a>, leaves and shoots are cut from the plant periodically to ensure softness. Harvested shoots are first boiled in <a href="/wiki/Lye" title="Lye">lye</a> to prepare fibers for <a href="/wiki/Yarn" title="Yarn">yarn</a>-making. These banana shoots produce fibers of varying degrees of softness, yielding yarns and textiles with differing qualities for specific uses. For example, the outermost fibers of the shoots are the coarsest, and are suitable for <a href="/wiki/Tablecloth" title="Tablecloth">tablecloths</a>, while the softest innermost fibers are desirable for <a href="/wiki/Kimono" title="Kimono">kimono</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hakama" title="Hakama">kamishimo</a>. This traditional Japanese cloth-making process requires many steps, all performed by hand.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Banana_paper" title="Banana paper">Banana paper</a> can be made either from the <a href="/wiki/Bark_(botany)" title="Bark (botany)">bark</a> of the banana plant, mainly for artistic purposes, or from the fibers of the stem and non-usable fruits. The paper may be hand-made or industrially processed.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 208.66666666667px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 206.66666666667px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Het_verpakken_van_manilla-hennep_(musa_textilis)_in_balen_op_onderneming_Kali_Telepak_Besoeki_Oost-Java_TMnr_10011535.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Packing Manila hemp (Musa textilis) into bales, Java"><img alt="Packing Manila hemp (Musa textilis) into bales, Java" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Het_verpakken_van_manilla-hennep_%28musa_textilis%29_in_balen_op_onderneming_Kali_Telepak_Besoeki_Oost-Java_TMnr_10011535.jpg/310px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Het_verpakken_van_manilla-hennep_%28musa_textilis%29_in_balen_op_onderneming_Kali_Telepak_Besoeki_Oost-Java_TMnr_10011535.jpg" decoding="async" width="207" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Het_verpakken_van_manilla-hennep_%28musa_textilis%29_in_balen_op_onderneming_Kali_Telepak_Besoeki_Oost-Java_TMnr_10011535.jpg/465px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Het_verpakken_van_manilla-hennep_%28musa_textilis%29_in_balen_op_onderneming_Kali_Telepak_Besoeki_Oost-Java_TMnr_10011535.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Het_verpakken_van_manilla-hennep_%28musa_textilis%29_in_balen_op_onderneming_Kali_Telepak_Besoeki_Oost-Java_TMnr_10011535.jpg/619px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Het_verpakken_van_manilla-hennep_%28musa_textilis%29_in_balen_op_onderneming_Kali_Telepak_Besoeki_Oost-Java_TMnr_10011535.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="509" /></a></span></div>

		</li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses">Other uses</span></h3>
<p>The large leaves of bananas may also be used as <a href="/wiki/Umbrella" title="Umbrella">umbrellas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-morton_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morton-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Banana_peel" title="Banana peel">Banana peel</a> may have capability to extract <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_(chemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Heavy metal (chemistry)">heavy metal</a> <a href="/wiki/Contamination" title="Contamination">contamination</a> from river water, similar to other <a href="/wiki/Water_purification" title="Water purification">purification</a> materials.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
Waste bananas can be used to feed <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As with all living things, potassium-containing bananas emit <a href="/wiki/Radioactivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioactivity">radioactivity</a> at low levels occurring naturally from the <a href="/wiki/Potassium-40" title="Potassium-40">potassium-40</a> (K-40) isotope.<sup id="cite_ref-frame_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frame-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Banana_equivalent_dose" title="Banana equivalent dose">banana equivalent dose</a> of radiation was developed in 1995 as a simple teaching-tool to educate the public about the natural, small amount of K-40 radiation occurring in everyone and in common foods.<sup id="cite_ref-bed_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bed-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-edwards_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-edwards-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_roles">Cultural roles</span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:River_Kaveri_worship_Tiruchirappalli.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/River_Kaveri_worship_Tiruchirappalli.jpg/220px-River_Kaveri_worship_Tiruchirappalli.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="138" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/River_Kaveri_worship_Tiruchirappalli.jpg/330px-River_Kaveri_worship_Tiruchirappalli.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/River_Kaveri_worship_Tiruchirappalli.jpg/440px-River_Kaveri_worship_Tiruchirappalli.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1592" data-file-height="998" /></a><figcaption>Coconut, banana and banana leaves used while worshiping <a href="/wiki/Kaveri_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Kaveri River">River Kaveri</a> at <a href="/wiki/Tiruchirappalli" title="Tiruchirappalli">Tiruchirappalli</a>, India</figcaption></figure>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts">Arts</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a> poet <a href="/wiki/Matsuo_Bash%C5%8D" title="Matsuo Bashō">Matsuo Bashō</a> is named after the Japanese word 芭蕉 (<span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja">Bashō</i></span>) for the <a href="/wiki/Musa_basjoo" title="Musa basjoo">Japanese banana</a>. The <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja">Bashō</i></span> planted in his garden by a grateful student became a source of inspiration to his poetry, as well as a symbol of his life and home.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The song "<a href="/wiki/Yes!_We_Have_No_Bananas" title="Yes! We Have No Bananas">Yes! We Have No Bananas</a>" was written by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Silver" title="Frank Silver">Frank Silver</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irving_Cohn" title="Irving Cohn">Irving Cohn</a> and originally released in 1923; for many decades, it was the best-selling <a href="/wiki/Sheet_music" title="Sheet music">sheet music</a> in history. Since then the song has been rerecorded several times and has been particularly popular during banana shortages.<sup id="cite_ref-shaw_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shaw-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Koeppel_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koeppel-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A person slipping on a <a href="/wiki/Banana_peel" title="Banana peel">banana peel</a> has been a staple of <a href="/wiki/Physical_comedy" title="Physical comedy">physical comedy</a> for generations. An American comedy recording from 1910 features a popular character of the time, "Uncle Josh", claiming to describe his own such incident.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The cover artwork for <a href="/wiki/The_Velvet_Underground_%26_Nico" title="The Velvet Underground &amp; Nico">the debut album</a> of <a href="/wiki/The_Velvet_Underground" title="The Velvet Underground">The Velvet Underground</a> features a banana made by <a href="/wiki/Andy_Warhol" title="Andy Warhol">Andy Warhol</a>. On the original vinyl LP version, the design allowed the listener to "peel" this banana to find a pink, peeled phallic banana on the inside.<sup id="cite_ref-demain_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-demain-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Italian artist <a href="/wiki/Maurizio_Cattelan" title="Maurizio Cattelan">Maurizio Cattelan</a> created a concept art piece titled <i>Comedian</i><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> involving taping a banana to a wall using silver duct tape. The piece was exhibited briefly at the Art Basel in Miami before being removed from the exhibition and eaten without permission in another artistic stunt titled <i>Hungry Artist</i> by New York artist <a href="/wiki/David_Datuna" title="David Datuna">David Datuna</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion_and_folklore">Religion and folklore</span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:XRF-Tani-.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/XRF-Tani-.jpg/170px-XRF-Tani-.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="239" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/XRF-Tani-.jpg/255px-XRF-Tani-.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/XRF-Tani-.jpg/340px-XRF-Tani-.jpg 2x" data-file-width="588" data-file-height="828" /></a><figcaption><i><a href="/wiki/Nang_Tani" title="Nang Tani">Nang Tani</a></i>, the female <a href="/wiki/Ghost" title="Ghost">ghost</a> of Thai folklore that haunts banana plants</figcaption></figure>
<p>In India, bananas serve a prominent part in many festivals and occasions of <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindus</a>. In South Indian weddings, particularly <a href="/wiki/Tamil_culture" title="Tamil culture">Tamil weddings</a>, banana trees are tied in pairs to form an <a href="/wiki/Arch" title="Arch">arch</a> as a blessing to the couple for a long-lasting, useful life.<sup id="cite_ref-mirror_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mirror-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-earth_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-earth-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>In Thailand, it is believed that <a href="/wiki/Musa_balbisiana" title="Musa balbisiana">a certain type</a> of banana plant may be inhabited by a spirit, <a href="/wiki/Nang_Tani" title="Nang Tani">Nang Tani</a>, a type of ghost related to trees and similar plants that manifests itself as a young woman.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Often people tie a length of colored satin cloth around the pseudostem of the banana plants.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_Malays" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic Malays">Malay folklore</a>, the ghost known as <a href="/wiki/Pontianak_(folklore)" class="mw-redirect" title="Pontianak (folklore)">Pontianak</a> is associated with banana plants (<i>pokok pisang</i>), and its spirit is said to reside in them during the day.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
